Who is Exempt from Friday Prayer? (Hanafi Exemptions)
Rulings on who is exempt from Jumu'ah obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five categories exempt from Jumu'ah and explain the legal rationale for each exemption according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Jumu'ah is obligatory on every free, resident, male Muslim but is waived for those facing genuine hardship.

This lesson examines the categories of people who are excused from attending Friday prayer. The Hanafi school identifies five main exemptions: traveler, woman, sick person, slave, and one nursing a patient. Each exemption is based on a clear principle: the obligation of Jumu'ah is lifted when performance would cause undue hardship or conflict with a higher duty.

Guided Path
1
State the basic obligation
Recognize that Jumu'ah prayer is a time-specific obligation (wajib) upon every free, adult, male Muslim who is a resident (not traveling).
The obligation of Jumu'ah is established by Quran and Sunnah.
2
Identify the exempt categories
From the text, list the five categories: traveler, woman, sick person, slave, and nursing attendant (mumarrid).
Each category has a specific rationale.
3
Analyze the rationale for each
For each exemption, explain the underlying reason: hardship (traveler), prohibition of unnecessary outing (woman), incapacity (sick), service to master (slave), and care for patient (nurse).
The reasons reflect the principle of removing hardship.
4
Resolve sub-issues
Note the intra-Hanafi differences on mukatab (slave under contract), ma'dhun (slave permitted to trade), and part-free slave. Identify the most correct (asahh) position.
The mufta bihi is that Jumu'ah is obligatory on the mukatab and part-free slave, but not on the ma'dhun.
5
Apply the ruling
In a given scenario, determine whether Jumu'ah is obligatory by checking the individual's status against these conditions.
The exemption applies only if the person genuinely falls into one of the categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تجب الجمعة على مسافر، ولا امرأة، ولا مريض، ولا عبد ..
قوله: (ولا تجب الجمعة على مسافر)؛ لأنه تلحقه المشقة بأدائها؛ ينقطع بانتظار الإمام عن سفره، فسقطت عنه كالصوم.
قوله: (ولا امرأة)؛ لأنها منهية عن الخروج، ومشغولة بخدمة الزوج.
قوله: (ولا مريض)؛ لعجزه عن ذلك. وأما المُمَرِّضُ: فالأصح أنه إن بقي المريض ضائعاً بخروجه: لم تجب عليه.
قوله: (ولا عبد)؛ لأنه مشغول بخدمة مولاه، فإن أذن له مولاه: وجبت عليه. وقال بعضهم: يخير.
- وهل تجب على المكاتب؟ قال بعضهم: نعم، وقال بعضهم: لا، والأصح الوجوب. وكذا معتق البعض في حال سعايته: كالمكاتب. وأما المأذون: فلا تجب عليه. كذا في «الفتاوى».
And Jumu'ah is not obligatory upon a traveler, nor a woman, nor a sick person, nor a slave. He said: (And Jumu'ah is not obligatory upon a traveler) because hardship afflicts him in performing it; he is cut off by waiting for the imam from his travel, so it is dropped from him like fasting. He said: (Nor a woman) because she is prohibited from going out and is occupied with serving her husband. He said: (Nor a sick person) due to his inability to do that. As for the one who is nursing (a patient): the most correct is that if the patient is left unattended by his leaving, it is not obligatory upon him. He said: (Nor a slave) because he is occupied with serving his master. If his master permits him, then it becomes obligatory upon him. And some said: he has a choice. Is it obligatory upon a mukatab (slave under contract of manumission)? Some said: yes, and some said: no, and the most correct is obligation. Similarly a part-free slave in the period of working off his freedom is like a mukatab. As for the ma'dhun (slave permitted to trade), it is not obligatory upon him. Thus in al-Fatawa.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage lists the categories of people who are not obligated to attend Friday prayer. The reasons: a traveler would face hardship and disruption of journey; a woman is advised to stay home and is occupied with domestic duties; a sick person is unable; a slave is occupied with serving his master. Sub-issues: a person nursing a sick patient is exempt if the patient would be neglected. For a slave, if the master grants permission, Jumu'ah becomes obligatory. For a mukatab (a slave who has made a contract to purchase his freedom), the majority (and the most correct view) says Jumu'ah is obligatory because he is financially independent. Similarly, a part-free slave working for freedom is like a mukatab. A ma'dhun slave (authorized to trade) is not obligated, possibly because he is still a slave in status. These rulings reflect the principle that Jumu'ah is a communal obligation that is waived in cases of genuine need or incapacity.

Key Terms
مسافر (musafir): A traveler undertaking a journey of at least three days (approx. 48 miles) according to Hanafi law.
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has entered a contract (kitabah) to earn money and purchase his freedom in installments.
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A slave who is permitted by his master to engage in trade; he retains slave status but has financial autonomy.
ممرض (mumarrid): A person who is nursing a sick patient; if the patient would be left unattended, this person is exempt from Jumu'ah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A traveler
B) A woman
C) A sick person
D) A freeman residing in town

Key Takeaway
Jumu'ah is not obligatory upon those who face genuine hardship: traveler, woman, sick, slave, and nursing attendant; the obligation for slaves with special status depends on their degree of freedom.

Exemptions from Friday Prayer and the Ruling on Leading
Commentary listing exemptions and leadership ruling with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the categories exempted from Friday prayer in Hanafi fiqh, identify the underlying cause (illah) for each exemption, and explain why such persons may validly lead Friday prayer even when not obligated.

Core Idea
Hardship excuses from obligation, but the prayer itself remains valid and can be led.

This passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira explains the famous exemptions from Friday prayer: traveler, woman, sick, slave, blind, and others. More importantly, it reveals a key Hanafi principle: exemption from an obligation does not negate the validity of the act. Even those excused may attend and lead the prayer, a view contested by Zufar but upheld by the majority of Hanafi masters.

Guided Path
1
List the exempted categories
Identify each category mentioned in the text: traveler, woman, sick, slave, blind, caregiver (mumarrid), mukatab, part-free (muʿtaq baʿḍ), ma'dhun (slave permitted to trade), hired laborer, one hiding from an unjust ruler, and those with excuse of rain/mud.
The mukatab and part-free are obligated according to the most correct view, contrary to some.
2
Identify the illah (cause) for each exemption
The text gives a reason for each: traveler (hardship of waiting), woman (prohibition from leaving home, busy with husband), sick (inability), slave (occupied with master's service), blind (difficulty of movement).
The common thread is hardship (mashaqqa) that makes the obligation burdensome.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on the blind
Abu Hanifa says blind is exempt even with a guide; Sahibayn say obligatory if a guide is found. The text supports Abu Hanifa's view by analogy to the elderly.
This dispute hinges on whether the guide removes the hardship entirely.
4
Analyze the ruling on leading Friday prayer
The text states that travelers, slaves, sick, and the like may lead Friday prayer. Zufar objects because it is not obligatory for them, like children/women. The Hanafi response: the address (khitab) includes them, but they are excused; if their prayer were invalid, their attendance would be futile.
This principle extends to any validly performed prayer: even if not originally wajib, the act itself is accepted.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider a traveler who passes through a city on Friday. He is not obligated to attend, but if he does and prays with the congregation, it suffices for his Zuhr prayer. He may also be chosen as imam if he is qualified.
The sufficiency (ijza') applies even to those not obligated, as long as they perform the prayer correctly.

Chapter of Friday Prayer. Friday prayer is not obligatory on a traveler, nor a woman, nor a sick person, nor a slave... Al-Shafi'i said: It is recommended to recite Surah al-Jumu'ah in the first rak'ah and Surah al-Munafiqun in the second. Because his statement: (Friday prayer is not obligatory on a traveler) because hardship affects him by performing it; he is interrupted by waiting for the imam from his travel, so it is dropped for him, like fasting. His statement: (nor) a woman because she is prohibited from going out and is busy serving her husband. His statement: (nor a sick person) due to his inability. As for the caregiver (mumarrid), the most correct is that if the patient would be left unattended by his going out, it is not obligatory. His statement: (nor a slave) because he is busy serving his master; if his master permits him, it becomes obligatory. Some said: he is given a choice. And is it obligatory on a mukatab? Some said yes, some said no, and the most correct is obligation. Similarly, a part-free person during his effort to earn freedom is like a mukatab. As for a ma'dhun (slave permitted to trade), it is not obligatory. Thus in al-Fatawa. (1) Mughni al-Muhtaj 290/1. Nor a blind person. If they attend and pray with the people, it suffices them for the obligatory prayer of the time. And it is permissible for a traveler, slave, sick person, and the like to lead in Friday prayer. His statement: (nor on a blind person) even if he finds a guide, according to Abu Hanifa. And according to them (the two companions): if he finds a guide, it becomes obligatory because he is able to walk, only he lacks guidance. And for Abu Hanifa: that effort (sa'y) is difficult for him, so he resembles an elderly person. Similarly, a hired laborer does not go to Friday prayer and congregation except with permission of the employer. Abu Ali al-Daqqaq said: He does not have the right to prevent him, but he deducts from the wage proportionally. Similarly, it is not obligatory on one who is hidden from an unjust ruler. And it is also dropped due to the excuse of rain and mud. His statement: (If they attend and pray with the people, it suffices them for the obligatory prayer of the time) because they undertook it, so they become like a traveler when he fasts. His statement: (And it is permissible for a traveler, slave, sick person, and the like to lead in Friday prayer). And Zufar said: It is not permissible because it is not obligatory upon them, so they resemble a boy and a woman. And for us: that the address includes them, except they are excused to remove hardship; for if the obligatory prayer of the time were not dropped from them by their performing Friday prayer, there would be corruption of the situation, because
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the Mukhtasar al-Quduri, explaining the chapter of Friday prayer (Salat al-Jumu'ah). The author first lists the categories of people on whom Friday prayer is not obligatory: a traveler, a woman, a sick person, a slave, and a blind person. The commentary then provides the rationale for each exemption. For the traveler, the hardship of waiting for the imam interrupts his journey, similar to the exemption from fasting. The woman is exempt because she is commanded to stay home and is busy with household duties. The sick person is excused due to inability. The caregiver of a sick person is also exempt if the patient would be neglected. For the slave, he is busy with his master's service, but if the master permits, it becomes obligatory. There is a difference of opinion regarding the mukatab (a slave contracted for freedom) and a part-free person; the most correct view is that it is obligatory. The blind person is exempt according to Abu Hanifa even if he has a guide, due to the difficulty of going, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it becomes obligatory if a guide is available. The passage then discusses other exemptions: a hired laborer needs permission, one in hiding from an unjust ruler is exempt, and rain or mud are valid excuses. Importantly, if any of these exempted persons attend Friday prayer and pray with the congregation, it suffices for their obligatory prayer of the time (Zuhr). They are also permitted to lead the Friday prayer as imam. Zufar disagreed on this permissibility, analogizing to children and women who cannot lead, but the Hanafi response is that the address (khitab) includes them, and they are only excused due to hardship, so their participation is valid and effective.

Key Terms
مسافر (musāfir): A traveler who has left his residence for a journey of three days or more (according to Hanafi fiqh) and is exempt from Friday prayer.
مريض (marīḍ): A seriously ill person for whom attending Friday prayer would cause harm or hardship.
عبد (ʿabd): An enslaved male (or female) who is owned by another and busy serving his master.
أعمى (aʿmā): A blind person who cannot see the path; includes one who can find a guide.
المأذون (al-maʾdhūn): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade; such a slave is not obligated for Friday prayer.
المكاتب (al-mukātab): A slave who has entered a contract to earn his freedom by paying instalments.
معتق البعض (muʿtaq al-baʿḍ): A slave who has been partially freed, still working to earn the remaining portion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Exemption from obligation due to hardship does not invalidate the act; those excused from Friday prayer may still attend and lead, and their prayer fulfills the obligatory Zuhr.

Exemptions from Friday Prayer: The Blind and Others
Commentary on exemption of blind and others from Friday prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on Friday prayer obligation for a blind person with a guide, distinguish Abu Hanifa's view from the Sahibayn, and identify other legitimate excuses for non-attendance.

Core Idea
A blind person is exempt from Jumu'ah even with a guide because hardship is the 'illa, not sight.

This passage from al-Jawharah al-Nayirah addresses who is excused from Friday prayer. The main dispute is over a blind person: Imam Abu Hanifa holds he is exempt in all cases, while his two disciples oblige him if he has a guide. The discussion illustrates how hardship (mashaqqah) operates as a legal cause for exemption. Beyond the blind, other excuses are also mentioned.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
What is the act being ruled upon? Friday prayer (salat al-Jumu'ah) is fard 'ayn. The dispute concerns whether a blind person is exempt from this obligation.
The matn says 'not upon a blind person', but the commentary reveals a split.
2
Note the opposing positions
Abu Hanifa holds the blind are exempt even if they have a guide. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold the obligation returns if a guide is available.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze the evidence for each
Abu Hanifa uses qiyas: the blind person is like a disabled person (zamin) because attending is difficult. The Sahibayn argue that ability to walk with a guide makes him capable, similar to a sighted person who cannot see the imam.
The shared 'illa is hardship, but they differ on its presence.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if the blind person does not attend, according to Abu Hanifa there is no sin; according to the Sahibayn, if he had a guide, he sins.
This difference matters for fatwa and personal accountability.
5
Apply to other excuses
The passage then lists other exemptions: hired worker (without employer's permission), person hiding from an unjust ruler, and excuses of rain and mud. Each is based on hardship.
These are all applications of the same principle of raf' al-haraj.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب صلاة الجمعة
ولا أعمى.
* قوله: (ولا على أعمى) ولو وجد قائداً عند أبي حنيفة. وعندهما: إذا وجد قائداً وجبت عليه ؛ لأنه قادر على المشي، وإنما لا يهتدي.
ولأبي حنيفة : أنه يشق عليه السعي، فأشبه الزمن.
وكذا الأجير : لا يذهب إلى الجمعة والجماعة إلا بإذن المستأجر. وقال أبو علي الدقاق: ليس له منعه، لكن يُسقط من الأجرة بقسطه.
وكذا لا يجب على المختفي من الأمير الظالم.

Chapter of Friday Prayer. And not upon a blind person. * His saying: (And not upon a blind person) even if he finds a guide according to Abu Hanifa. And according to the two, if he finds a guide, it is obligatory upon him because he is able to walk; he merely is not guided. And for Abu Hanifa: that it is difficult for him to go, so he is like a disabled person. And likewise the hired worker: he does not go to Friday prayer and congregation except with the permission of the employer. And Abu Ali al-Daqqaq said: He cannot prevent him, but he reduces from the wages proportionally. And likewise it is not obligatory upon the one hiding from the unjust ruler. And it is also nullified due to the excuse of rain and mud.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage addresses exemptions from the obligation of Friday prayer. The commentary begins by explaining the matn's phrase 'not upon a blind person.' According to Imam Abu Hanifa, Friday prayer is not obligatory on a blind person even if he has a guide, because the hardship (mashaqqah) of going to the mosque remains. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that if he has a guide, the obligation returns because he is able to walk; his lack of sight does not prevent him from attending with assistance. Abu Hanifa's position is strengthened by analogy (qiyas) to a disabled person (zamin) for whom hardship is excused. The passage then lists other exemptions: a hired worker must not go to Friday prayer without the employer's permission; if he does go, the employer may deduct from his wages proportionally (view of Abu Ali al-Daqqaq). Additionally, a person hiding from a tyrannical ruler is excused, as are those who face rain or mud. These exemptions are based on the principle of removing hardship (raf' al-haraj).

Key Terms
أعمى (a'ma): A blind person, used here to mean one who is visually impaired to the extent that attending Friday prayer is difficult.
قائد (qa'id): A guide who leads the blind person.
زمن (zamin): A disabled person who is physically weak or incapacitated.
أجير (ajir): A hired worker, whose obligation to attend Friday prayer is conditional on employer's permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because he is not considered a mukallaf.
D) Because he cannot perform the prayer correctly.

Key Takeaway
The exemption from Jumu'ah for the blind is established by qiyas on hardship, but the Sahibayn add a condition of having a guide; other excuses follow the same principle.

Sufficiency of Friday Prayer for Non-Obliged: The Traveler's Fast Analogy
Ruling with proof via qiyas jaliy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that voluntary attendance of Friday prayer by non-obliged persons suffices for Zuhr and explain the qiyas (analogy) to a traveler fasting.

Core Idea
Voluntary undertaking of Friday prayer substitutes for Zuhr, just as a traveler's voluntary fast substitutes for Ramadan.

This lesson unpacks a concise Hanafi rule: those not obliged to attend Friday prayer—such as the blind or sick—if they do attend and pray with the congregation, it fulfills their Zuhr obligation. The proof is a sharp qiyas: the voluntary act of worship (Friday prayer) replaces the underlying obligation (Zuhr), just as a traveler who voluntarily fasts fulfills his Ramadan obligation. We will dissect this analogy step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Locate the exact ruling in the passage: attendance of non-obliged persons at Friday prayer suffices for Zuhr (farḍ al-waqt). Note that the ruling applies only if they actually pray with the congregation.
The key phrase is 'أجزأهم عن فرض الوقت'.
2
Identify the Proof (Dalīl)
The text gives one proof: 'because they undertook it (tahammaluhu)'. This is a qiyas (analogy). The implicit analogy is to a traveler who is not obliged to fast but may voluntarily fast and it counts.
Look for the comparative particle 'ka-' (like) in the commentary.
3
Dissect the Qiyas
Break down the analogy: Aṣl (original case) = traveler voluntarily fasting; Farʿ (new case) = non-obliged person attending Friday prayer. Find the ʿilla (effective cause) that links them. The common ʿilla is voluntary undertaking of a more complete worship that substitutes for the standard obligation.
The ʿilla is not explicitly stated but implied by the term 'tahammaluhu' (they undertook).
4
Apply to Other Scenarios
Generalize: whenever a person not originally obliged performs an act of worship voluntarily and correctly, it can fulfil the underlying obligation. Consider cases like a traveler praying Jumuʿah or a woman performing Hajj without a mahram (where permissible).
This principle is not absolute; it applies only when the voluntary act is a valid substitute (badal).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
* قوله : (فإن حضروا وصلوا مع الناس: أجزأهم عن فرض الوقت)؛ لأنهم تحملوه، فصاروا كالمسافر إذا صام.
* His saying: (So if they attend and pray with the people, it suffices them from the obligatory time) because they undertook it, so they become like the traveler when he fasts.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage explains that even though certain individuals (like the blind, sick, etc.) are not originally obliged to attend Friday prayer, if they do attend and pray the Friday prayer with the congregation, it suffices for the obligatory prayer of that time (i.e., Zuhr). The reason given is that they have undertaken the Friday prayer, which is a more complete form of worship. The analogy is made to a traveler who is not obliged to fast in Ramadan, but if he chooses to fast, it counts for him. Similarly, here, the Friday prayer replaces the Zuhr obligation.

Key Terms
تحملوه (tahammaluhu): They undertook it (the Friday prayer) voluntarily.
فرض الوقت (fard al-waqt): The obligatory prayer of that time (i.e., Zuhr).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It suffices for his Zuhr obligation.
C) It is invalid because he is not obliged.
D) He must repeat it later.

Key Takeaway
Voluntary performance of Friday prayer by non-obliged persons replaces the Zuhr obligation, analogized to a traveler's voluntary fast.

Who May Lead Friday Prayer: Traveler, Slave, Sick Person
Commentary on permissibility of non-resident free persons leading Friday prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on travelers, slaves, and sick persons leading Friday prayer, and explain why they are counted in the number of followers.

Core Idea
Excused obligation does not negate capacity; excluding them contradicts the purpose of exemption.

Friday prayer has specific requirements, including a minimum number of followers and the imam being a resident free male? This lesson examines a key Hanafi view: even those not personally obliged—travelers, slaves, sick—may lead and be counted. We will analyze the arguments of Zufar and Shafi'i, and understand the legal reasoning (fasad al-wad') that supports the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
State the issue and the Hanafi position
The matn says 'it is permissible for the traveler, the slave, the sick, and similar to lead in Friday prayer.' Identify the ruling: permissible (ja'iz) and the people involved.
The Hanafi school holds that such individuals can be imams for Friday prayer.
2
Analyze Zufar's objection
Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) argues it is not permissible because Friday prayer is not obligatory on them. He analogizes to a child or woman, who cannot lead. Understand the analogy and its basis.
Zufar's qiyas: non-obligation → inability to lead.
3
Examine the Hanafi response using fasad al-wad'
The Hanafi counter-argument: the command (khitab) addresses them, but they are excused due to hardship. If their participation did not fulfill the obligation, it would create a contradiction (fasad al-wad')—the exemption would lose its purpose. Disallowing them would increase hardship.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on legal coherence.
4
Distinguish child and woman
The text explains why child and woman are different: a child's prayer is not obligatory (so leading would base obligation on supererogation); a woman cannot lead men due to other rules. So the analogy with traveler/slave/sick fails.
These exceptions preserve the principle without inconsistency.
5
Address the counting dispute with Shafi'i
The text concludes that if Friday prayer is valid with them as followers, they count toward the minimum number. Shafi'i allows them to lead but does not count them. Understand the thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafi counts them; Shafi'i does not.
Practical consequence: the required quorum may be achieved with such followers in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
* قوله: (ويجوز للمسافر والعبد والمريض ونحوهم أن يؤموا في الجمعة).
وقال زفر : لا يجوز؛ لأنه لا فرض عليهم، فأشبهوا الصبي والمرأة. ولنا: أن الخطاب يتناولهم، إلا أنهم عُذروا؛ دفعاً للحرج، فلو لم يسقط عنهم فرض الوقت بأدائهم الجمعة : كان فيه فساد الوضع؛ لأن الإسقاط عنهم لدفع الحرج.
والقول بعدم الجواز : يؤدي إلى الحرج.
:
وأما الصبي فلا يقع فعله فرضاً، فيكون فيه بناء الفرض على النفل، فلذلك لا يجوز.
وأما المرأة فلا تصلح لإمامة الرجال.
:
وإذا ثبت انعقاد الجمعة بائتمامهم: اعتد بهم في عدد المؤتمين، كالحر المقيم. وقال الشافعي : يجوز أن يكونوا أئمةً، ولا يُعتد بهم في العدد.
* His saying: (And it is permissible for the traveler, the slave, the sick person, and the like to lead in Friday prayer). And Zufar said: It is not permissible because there is no obligation upon them, so they are like the child and the woman. And for us: The address (of Friday prayer) includes them, but they are excused to remove hardship. So if their performance of Friday prayer did not lift the obligation of the time from them, it would be a corruption of the situation because the exemption for them is to remove hardship. And the saying that it is not permissible leads to hardship. As for the child, his action does not occur as an obligation, so it would be building the obligatory upon the supererogatory, therefore it is not permissible. As for the woman, she is not fit to lead men. And if the validity of Friday prayer by their following is established, they are counted in the number of followers, like the free resident. And al-Shafi'i said: It is permissible for them to be leaders, but they are not counted in the number.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage addresses who is qualified to lead the Friday prayer. The matn states that a traveler, a slave, a sick person, and similar individuals may lead. Zufar argues they cannot because Friday prayer is not obligatory upon them, likening them to children and women. The Hanafi refutation is that the command to establish Friday prayer addresses all qualified individuals, but some are excused due to hardship. If their attendance did not fulfill the obligation, it would create a contradiction (fasad al-wad'), because the very exemption is based on removing hardship. Disallowing them to lead would increase hardship. The passage then distinguishes between children and women: a child's prayer is not considered obligatory (since he is not yet mukallaf), so his leading would involve basing an obligatory prayer on a supererogatory act, which is invalid. A woman is unfit to lead men due to other considerations. Finally, it notes that once the Friday prayer is valid with these individuals as followers, they count towards the minimum number of followers required. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees on this point: he allows them to lead but does not count them in the number (possibly because he considers them not fully obliged).

Key Terms
يؤم (ya'ummu): To lead the prayer as an imam.
الخطاب (al-khitab): The address of the command (to pray Friday).
فساد الوضع (fasad al-wad'): Corruption of the legal structure; an inconsistency in the law.
اعتد بهم في عدد المؤتمين (i'tadda bihim fi 'adad al-mu'tamin): They are counted in the number of followers (required for Friday prayer).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the sick person is not really excused.
B) Because the command to pray Friday includes him, and disallowing him would create hardship.
C) Because the sick person's prayer is supererogatory, so it benefits him.
D) Because the sick person can only lead if he is wealthy.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a traveler, slave, or sick person may lead Friday prayer and is counted in the number of followers, because their exemption does not negate the validity of their prayer and excluding them would defeat the purpose of the exemption.

The Validity of Zuhr Prayer on Friday Before the Khutbah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on Zuhr before Friday prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on praying Zuhr at home on Friday before the Friday prayer, explain the basis for Zufar's dispute, and identify the practical consequence of the disagreement.

Core Idea
Zuhr on Friday before the Friday prayer is valid but makruh — valid because the original obligation is Zuhr, disliked for abandoning the congregation.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute about the status of Zuhr prayer on Friday before the Friday prayer commences. The majority Hanafi position holds that such a prayer is valid but disliked, while Zufar argues it is invalid until after the Friday prayer. The difference stems from whether the Friday prayer or Zuhr is considered the 'original' obligation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling as expressed in the text: the prayer is valid but makruh (disliked). Note the key terms 'kariha' and 'jazat'.
The primary authority is the matn of Al-Quduri.
2
Identify the opponent and his view
Zufar disagrees, holding that the Zuhr prayer is invalid (la yujzihu) until after the imam completes the Friday prayer.
Zufar is a prominent Hanafi jurist; this is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze Zufar's proof
Zufar argues that Friday is the original obligation (al-farida aslan) and Zuhr is a substitute (badal). One cannot resort to a substitute when able to perform the original.
This reasoning is based on the principle of preferring the original over the substitute.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds that the original obligation for all people is Zuhr; Friday is a replacement for those who attend. The hadith that Zuhr time begins at zawal supports that the time for Zuhr is valid before Friday prayer.
The Hanafi position prioritizes the general obligation of Zuhr.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: according to the Hanafi view, the prayer is valid (though disliked); according to Zufar, it is invalid and must be repeated after Friday.
The mufta bihi is the majority Hanafi view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
* قوله: (ومن صلى الظهر في منزله يوم الجمعة قبل صلاة الإمام، ولا عُذر به كره له ذلك، وجازت صلاته).
وقال زفر : لا يجزيه الظهر، إلا بعد فراغ الإمام من الجمعة؛ لأن أصله : أن الجمعة هي الفريضة أصلاً، والظهر كالبدل، ولا يُصار إلى البدل مع القدرة على الأصل.
ولنا: أن أصل الفرض هو الظهر في حق الكافة، وهذا هو الظاهر من الدليل، قال عليه الصلاة والسلام: «أول وقت الظهر حين تزول الشمس».
* His saying: (And whoever prays Zuhr in his house on Friday before the imam's prayer, and has no excuse, it is disliked for him and his prayer is valid). And Zufar said: His Zuhr is not sufficient until after the imam finishes from Friday prayer, because the origin is that Friday is the original obligation, and Zuhr is a substitute, and one does not resort to a substitute when able to perform the original. And for us: The original obligation is Zuhr for all people, and this is the apparent from the evidence. He (peace be upon him) said: 'The beginning of Zuhr time is when the sun declines.'
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling for a person who prays Zuhr at home on Friday before the imam begins the Friday prayer, without having a valid excuse. According to the Hanafi school, this act is makruh (disliked) but valid. Zufar argues that it is not valid at all because the Friday prayer is the primary obligation, and Zuhr is merely a substitute. He holds that one cannot resort to the substitute when the original is available. The Hanafi response is that the original obligation is Zuhr for everyone; Friday is a special obligation that replaces Zuhr for those who attend. Evidence is the hadith that the time for Zuhr begins at zawal. Since Friday prayer has not yet been performed, the time for Zuhr is valid, and one may pray it if he does not attend the Friday prayer. Therefore, the act is valid but disliked because he is abandoning the superior congregation.

Key Terms
كره له ذلك (kariha lahu dhalik): It is disliked (makruh) for him to do that.
جازت صلاته (jazat salatuhu): His prayer is valid (encompassing the obligatory Zuhr).
الظهر كالبدل (al-zuhr ka al-badal): Zuhr is like a substitute for Friday prayer when Friday prayer is not performed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Invalid and must be repeated.
B) Makruh but valid.
C) Recommended.
D) Obligatory.

Key Takeaway
Praying Zuhr at home on Friday before the imam's Friday prayer without an excuse is valid but disliked (makruh) in the Hanafi school.

Ruling on Praying Zuhr on Friday Before the Imam's Prayer
Explains the ruling and the underlying dispute about the obligatory prayer on Friday

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on praying Zuhr on Friday before the Imam's prayer, explain the evidence, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf from that of Zufar.

Core Idea
Zuhr remains the default obligation on Friday; praying it early is valid but disliked.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira addresses a practical scenario: a person who prays Zuhr at home on Friday before the Imam leads Jumu'ah, and without a valid excuse. The ruling is not binary—it balances validity with dislike. Understanding this requires grasping the Hanafi view that Zuhr is the original obligation (fard al-waqt) and Jumu'ah is a command to drop it. We will walk through the ruling, the proof, and the internal Hanafi disagreement with Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
Pinpoint the specific action: praying Zuhr in one's home on Friday before the Imam's prayer, with no excuse. State the hukm: valid (ja'iz) but disliked (makruh tanzih).
The dislike is due to abandoning the sunnah of attending Jumu'ah.
2
Locate the Dalil
Find the proof cited in the text. The author uses the hadith 'The first time of Zuhr is when the sun declines' to argue that the obligation of Zuhr remains for all days, including Friday.
This is a Sunni ahad hadith, so its thubut is zanni.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
Explain how the hadith proves the ruling. The hadith does not exclude Friday, so the general rule applies: Zuhr is the original obligation. The command to attend Jumu'ah is a command to drop this obligation, not to replace it.
Also note the rational argument: ability (tamakkun) to perform Zuhr individually exists, while Jumu'ah depends on conditions.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf
Identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: Zuhr is the original obligation. Muhammad: uncertain but either prayer drops the obligation. Zufar: Jumu'ah is the original; Zuhr is invalid before Jumu'ah ends.
The practical difference appears in intention: if one intends fard al-waqt in Jumu'ah, it is invalid according to Abu Hanifa.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi Tarjih
Determine the mufta bihi. The text favors the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf because it is supported by the hadith and avoids hardship (raf' al-haraj). This is the operative position for fatwa.
Zufar's view is considered weak due to causing hardship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب صلاة الجمعة
ومن صلى الظهر في منزله يومَ الجمعة قبل صلاة الإمام، ولا عُذر له : كره له ذلك، وجازت صلاته . الإسقاط عنهم لدفع الحرج. والقول بعدم الجواز : يؤدي إلى الحرج. : وأما الصبي فلا يقع فعله فرضاً، فيكون فيه بناء الفرض على النفل، فلذلك لا يجوز. وأما المرأة فلا تصلح لإمامة الرجال. : وإذا ثبت انعقاد الجمعة بائتمامهم: اعتد بهم في عدد المؤتمين، كالحر المقيم. وقال الشافعي (١) : يجوز أن يكونوا أئمةً، ولا يُعتد بهم في العدد. * قوله: (ومن صلى الظهر في منزله يوم الجمعة قبل صلاة الإمام، ولا عُذر به كره له ذلك، وجازت صلاته). من وقال زفر : لا يجزيه الظهر، إلا بعد فراغ الإمام من الجمعة؛ لأن أصله : أن الجمعة هي الفريضة أصلاً، والظهر كالبدل، ولا يُصار إلى البدل مع القدرة على الأصل. ولنا: أن أصل الفرض هو الظهر في حق الكافة، وهذا هو الظاهر من الدليل، قال عليه الصلاة والسلام: «أول وقت الظهر حين تزول الشمس» (٢). ولأجل حديث؟ (ولم يفصل بين هذا اليوم وغيره، إلا أنه مأمور بإسقاطه بأداء الجمعة. ولأن مبنى التكليف على التمكن، وهو متمكن من أداء الظهر بنفسه، دون الجمعة؛ لتوقفها على شرائط لا تتم به وحده، وعلى التمكن يدور التكليف. ولأنه إذا فات الوقت قضى الظهر، دون الجمعة. فإذا ثبت عندنا أن أصل الفرض هو الظهر، وقد أداه في وقته: أجزأه. وحاصله أن فرض الوقت عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف: الظهر، وقد أمر بإسقاطه بالجمعة. وقال محمد: لا أدري ما أصل فرض الوقت في هذا اليوم؟ ولكن يسقط عنه الفرض بأداء الظهر، أو الجمعة. يعني أن أصل الفرض أحدهما، لا بعينه، ويتعين بفعله. : وفائدته إذا أحرم للجمعة بنية فرض الوقت: لا يجوز عندنا؛ لأن فرض الوقت هو الظهر، ولا تتأدى الجمعة بنية الظهر، وعند زفر: يجوز؛ لأن فرض الوقت : الجمعة عنده، وقد نواها. وقوله : قبل صلاة الإمام : قيَّد بذلك ؛ احترازاً عن قول زفر، فإن عنده: لا يجزئه الظهر إلا بعد فراغ الإمام من صلاة الجمعة. كذا في «النهاية». ينظر التعريف والإخبار ١٠٠/١
Chapter of Friday Prayer. Whoever prays Zuhr in his house on Friday before the Imam's prayer, and has no excuse: it is disliked for him, and his prayer is valid. The dropping [of the obligation] from them to repel hardship. The statement of non-validity leads to hardship. As for the child, his action does not become obligatory, so it would be building an obligation on supererogation, therefore it is not permissible. As for the woman, she is not suitable for leading men. If it is established that Friday prayer is valid by their following, they are counted in the number of followers, like a free resident. Al-Shafi'i said: It is permissible for them to be imams, and they are not counted in the number. His statement: (Whoever prays Zuhr in his house on Friday before the Imam's prayer and has no excuse: it is disliked for him, and his prayer is valid). And Zufar said: Zuhr does not suffice him except after the Imam finishes Friday prayer; because the principle is that Friday is the original obligation, and Zuhr is like a substitute, and one does not resort to the substitute when capable of the original. Our proof: The original obligation is Zuhr for everyone, and this is evident from the evidence; he (peace be upon him) said: 'The first time of Zuhr is when the sun declines' (Hadith). And he did not differentiate between this day and others, except that he is commanded to drop it by performing Friday. And because the basis of obligation is ability, and he is able to perform Zuhr on his own, unlike Friday which depends on conditions not fulfilled by him alone, and obligation revolves around ability. And because if the time passes, he makes up Zuhr, not Friday. So if it is established with us that the original obligation is Zuhr, and he performed it in its time, it suffices him. The summary: The obligatory prayer of the time according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf is Zuhr, and he is commanded to drop it by performing Friday. Muhammad said: I do not know which is the original obligation of the time on this day? But the obligation is dropped by performing either Zuhr or Friday. Meaning that the original obligation is one of them, not specified, and it becomes specified by his action. Its benefit: If one starts Friday with the intention of the obligatory time prayer, it is not valid according to us because the obligatory time prayer is Zuhr, and Friday is not performed by intending Zuhr. According to Zufar, it is valid because the obligatory time prayer is Friday in his view. His statement: 'before the Imam's prayer' is qualified to avoid Zufar's view, for according to him, Zuhr does not suffice him except after the Imam finishes Friday prayer. This is in al-Nihaya. Refer to al-Ta'rif wa al-Ikhabar 1/100.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira is a commentary on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar, specifically the chapter on Friday prayer. The author explains the ruling for a person who prays Zuhr at home on Friday before the Imam leads the Jumu'ah prayer, and who has no legitimate excuse (such as illness or travel) for missing Jumu'ah. The ruling is that this action is makruh tanzihi (disliked) but the Zuhr prayer itself is valid and suffices for the obligation. The author then discusses the rationale: the obligation of Zuhr is dropped by performing Jumu'ah, but if one prays Zuhr before the Imam, it is valid because the original obligation is still Zuhr. The passage contrasts three positions: (1) Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the original obligation is Zuhr, and Jumu'ah is a command to drop it; (2) Muhammad al-Shaybani is uncertain which is the original but says either prayer drops the obligation; (3) Zufar holds that Jumu'ah is the original and Zuhr cannot be performed before Jumu'ah. The author provides proof from the hadith that the time for Zuhr begins when the sun declines, without excluding Friday, and rational arguments about ability (tamakkun). The practical difference appears in the issue of intention: if one begins Jumu'ah intending the obligatory time prayer (fard al-waqt), it is invalid for the Hanafis because the obligatory time is Zuhr, not Jumu'ah. The passage also briefly addresses the status of women and children in Jumu'ah: a child's prayer cannot be obligatory, so if a child leads, the followers would be building obligation on supererogation, which is invalid. A woman cannot lead men. If the Jumu'ah is valid with their following, they are counted in the number of followers, but al-Shafi'i disagrees and says they cannot be imams and are not counted in the number. The overall lesson underscores the Hanafi position that Zuhr is the default obligation on Friday, and Jumu'ah is a special substitute that takes precedence when conditions are met, but if one misses it, Zuhr suffices.

Key Terms
صلاة الجمعة (Salat al-Jumu'ah): The Friday congregational prayer that replaces Zuhr on Friday when conditions are met.
الظهر (al-Zuhr): The noon prayer, which is originally obligatory every day including Friday.
عذر (udhr): A legitimate excuse that exempts one from attending Jumu'ah, such as illness, travel, or fear.
فرض الوقت (fard al-waqt): The obligatory prayer of the specific time period; on Friday, it is either Zuhr or Jumu'ah according to different views.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, what is the ruling for someone who prays Zuhr in his house on Friday before the Imam's prayer without an excuse?
A) The prayer is invalid and must be repeated.
B) The prayer is valid and recommended.
C) The prayer is valid but disliked.
D) The prayer is invalid only if the person is a resident.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that Zuhr is the original obligation on Friday; praying it before Jumu'ah is valid but disliked without excuse.

The Original Obligation on Friday: A Hanafi Internal Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the obligation of Friday prayer and its consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute over the original obligation (asl al-fard) on Friday and apply the rulings to scenarios involving intending Jumu'ah after Zuhr.

Core Idea
The original obligation on Friday is Zuhr, discharged by Jumu'ah – but Muhammad and Zufar differ.

The Friday prayer is a weekly congregation, but what exactly is the obligation of the time? Is it Zuhr or Jumu'ah? This seemingly technical dispute has real consequences for intention and validity. In this lesson, we examine the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar, and trace the practical effects on a worshiper's prayer.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi majority position
Identify that Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that fard al-waqt on Friday is Zuhr, and the worshiper is commanded to discharge it by performing Jumu'ah. Understand the reasoning: ability (tamakkun) to perform Zuhr alone and the fact that if time passes, one makes up Zuhr, not Jumu'ah.
This is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position.
2
Identify the variant views
Note that Muhammad al-Shaybani says the original obligation is indeterminate (one of the two, not specified), and Zufar says it is Jumu'ah. Understand how these views affect the intention when praying Jumu'ah.
Muhammad's view leads to validity if one intends 'the obligation of the time' regardless of which prayer.
3
Follow the proof for the majority
Examine the arguments: obligation is based on ability (tamakkun), and the individual can perform Zuhr alone but not Jumu'ah (which requires congregation). Also, the makeup prayer (qada) is Zuhr, not Jumu'ah.
These analogical proofs (qiyas) are considered zanni al-dalala but are accepted in the school.
4
Apply to the intention scenario
Consider: if a person enters Jumu'ah intending 'fard al-waqt', is it valid? According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, no – because the obligation is Zuhr, not Jumu'ah. According to Zufar, yes – because he considers Jumu'ah as the obligation. Note the practical thamarah al-khilaf.
This intention issue is a key consequence of the dispute.

Source Text

باب صلاة الجمعة
ولم يفصل بين هذا اليوم وغيره، إلا أنه مأمور بإسقاطه بأداء الجمعة. ولأن مبنى التكليف على التمكن، وهو متمكن من أداء الظهر بنفسه، دون الجمعة؛ لتوقفها على شرائط لا تتم به وحده، وعلى التمكن يدور التكليف.
ولأنه إذا فات الوقت قضى الظهر، دون الجمعة.
فإذا ثبت عندنا أن أصل الفرض هو الظهر، وقد أداه في وقته: أجزأه. وحاصله أن فرض الوقت عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف: الظهر، وقد أمر بإسقاطه بالجمعة.
وقال محمد: لا أدري ما أصل فرض الوقت في هذا اليوم؟ ولكن يسقط عنه الفرض بأداء الظهر، أو الجمعة.
يعني أن أصل الفرض أحدهما، لا بعينه، ويتعين بفعله.
وفائدته إذا أحرم للجمعة بنية فرض الوقت: لا يجوز عندنا؛ لأن فرض الوقت هو الظهر، ولا تتأدى الجمعة بنية الظهر، وعند زفر: يجوز؛ لأن فرض الوقت : الجمعة عنده، وقد نواها.
وقوله : قبل صلاة الإمام : قيَّد بذلك ؛ احترازاً عن قول زفر، فإن عنده: لا يجزئه الظهر إلا بعد فراغ الإمام من صلاة الجمعة. كذا في «النهاية».
ينظر التعريف والإخبار ١٠٠/١.
فإن بدا له أن يحضر الجمعة فتوجه إليها : بطلت صلاة الظهر عند أبي حنيفة بالسعي.
وقوله: ولا عُذر به فلو كان به عُذر من الأعذار التي ذكرناها، فصلى الظهر، ثم شهد الجمعة : كانت الجمعة فرضه عندنا، وانقلب ظهره نفلاً؛ لأنه إذا شهدها: فهو والصحيح سواء.
وقال زفر: فرضه الظهر، ولم ينفسخ ؛ لأن الجمعة غير واجبة عليه، فوقعت الظهر موقع الفرض من غير إعادة.
وفائدته: إذا صلى المعذور، أو العبد الظهر في منزله، ثم دخل في الجمعة مع الإمام، فقبل أن يتم الإمام الجمعة : خرج وقت الظهر : فعندنا تلزمه إعادة الظهر؛ لأن ظهره الأول انقلبت نفلاً.
وعند زفر: لا تلزمه الإعادة؛ لأن هذا اليوم في حقه: كسائر الأيام، وفي سائرها لو صلى الظهر في بيته، ثم صلاها مع الجماعة : كان فرضه ما أداه في بيته، كذا هذا.
لكنا نقول: الجمعة أقوى من الظهر؛ لأنه يُشترط لها ما لا يشترط للظهر، ولا يظهر الضعيف في مقابلة القوي.
قوله: (فإن بدا له أن يحضر الجمعة، فتوجه إليها : بطلت صلاة الظهر عند أبي حنيفة بالسعي).
فإن صلى الجمعة: أجزأته، وإن لم يصلها: أعاد الظهر.
Chapter of Friday Prayer
And he did not differentiate between this day and others, except that he is commanded to discharge it by performing Jumu'ah. And because the basis of obligation is ability, and he is able to perform Zuhr by himself, unlike Jumu'ah; because it depends on conditions that are not fulfilled by him alone, and obligation revolves around ability. And because if the time passes, he makes up Zuhr, not Jumu'ah. So when it is established with us that the original obligation is Zuhr, and he performed it in its time: it suffices him. In summary, the obligatory of the time according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: Zuhr, and he is commanded to discharge it with Jumu'ah. And Muhammad said: I do not know what the original obligation of the time is on this day? But the obligation falls from him by performing Zuhr or Jumu'ah. Meaning that the original obligation is one of them, not specifically determined, and it becomes determined by his action. And its benefit: if he enters into Jumu'ah with the intention of the obligation of the time: it is not valid with us; because the obligation of the time is Zuhr, and Jumu'ah is not performed by intending Zuhr. And according to Zufar: it is valid; because the obligation of the time is Jumu'ah in his view, and he intended it. And his statement: before the Imam's prayer: he restricted it by that; as a precaution against the saying of Zufar, for according to him: Zuhr is not sufficient for him except after the Imam finishes the Jumu'ah prayer. Thus in 'al-Nihayah'. Refer to al-Ta'rif wa al-Ikhbar 100/1. Then if it occurs to him to attend Jumu'ah and he sets out for it: the Zuhr prayer becomes invalid according to Abu Hanifa by his going. And his statement: and there is no excuse in it: if there was an excuse from the excuses we mentioned, and he prayed Zuhr, then he attended Jumu'ah: then Jumu'ah becomes his obligation with us, and his Zuhr becomes supererogatory; because when he attends it: he is like the healthy person. And Zufar said: his obligation is Zuhr, and it is not dissolved; because Jumu'ah is not obligatory upon him, so Zuhr occurred as the obligation without repetition. And its benefit: if an excused person, or a slave, prays Zuhr in his house, then he enters into Jumu'ah with the Imam, and before the Imam completes Jumu'ah, the time of Zuhr exits: then with us: he is required to repeat Zuhr; because his first Zuhr was turned into supererogatory. And according to Zufar: he is not required to repeat; because this day for him is like other days, and in other days if he prayed Zuhr in his house, then he prayed it with the congregation: his obligation is what he performed in his house, similarly this. But we say: Jumu'ah is stronger than Zuhr; because conditions are required for it that are not required for Zuhr, and the weak does not appear in the face of the strong. His statement: (Then if it occurs to him to attend Jumu'ah, and he sets out for it: the Zuhr prayer becomes invalid according to Abu Hanifa by his going). If he prays Jumu'ah: it suffices him, and if he does not pray it: he repeats Zuhr.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the nature of the obligation on Friday. The author begins by establishing that the original obligation (asl al-fard) on Friday is Zuhr, based on the principle that obligation revolves around ability (tamakkun). One can perform Zuhr independently, whereas Jumu'ah requires conditions like congregation. Also, if the time for Jumu'ah passes, one makes up Zuhr, not Jumu'ah. Therefore, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the obligatory act of the time is Zuhr, and one is commanded to discharge it by performing Jumu'ah. Muhammad al-Shaybani differs: he does not specify which is the original obligation; rather, the obligation falls by performing either Zuhr or Jumu'ah, and the specific obligation is determined by whichever one actually performs. A practical consequence (fa'idah) is that if one enters Jumu'ah with the intention of the time's obligation (farḍ al-waqt), it is invalid according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf because the time's obligation is Zuhr, and Jumu'ah cannot be performed with the intention of Zuhr. Zufar, another Hanafi scholar, allows it because he considers Jumu'ah itself to be the obligation of the time. The author then notes that the condition 'before the Imam's prayer' (qabla salāt al-imām) in the matn is a precaution against Zufar's view that Zuhr is only sufficient after the Imam completes the Jumu'ah prayer. The passage continues with the case of one who prays Zuhr and then decides to attend Jumu'ah. In this case, going to Jumu'ah invalidates the Zuhr according to Abu Hanifa. If he actually prays Jumu'ah, it suffices; otherwise, he must repeat Zuhr. If one has a valid excuse (e.g., illness, travel) and prays Zuhr initially, then later attends Jumu'ah, Jumu'ah becomes his obligation and his Zuhr is turned into supererogatory prayer. Zufar disagrees, saying the Zuhr remains his obligation because Jumu'ah was not obligatory on him initially. The author concludes by asserting that Jumu'ah is stronger than Zuhr because it has more conditions, so the weaker (Zuhr) cannot stand in the presence of the stronger. Thus, the Hanafi ruling is that attending Jumu'ah after praying Zuhr invalidates the Zuhr, and Jumu'ah becomes obligatory.

Key Terms
فرض الوقت (farḍ al-waqt): The obligatory prayer of the time—on Friday, whether Zuhr or Jumu'ah is the specific obligation.
أصل الفرض (asl al-farḍ): The foundational original obligation, before considering its discharge by another act.
انقلب نفلاً (inqalaba nafalan): An obligatory prayer is transformed into supererogatory due to a subsequent act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a person prays Zuhr at home and then decides to attend Jumu'ah, what happens to the Zuhr prayer?
A) It remains valid and suffices.
B) It becomes invalid (batil) by his going to Jumu'ah.
C) It becomes supererogatory (nafl).
D) It must be repeated regardless of whether he prays Jumu'ah.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the original obligation on Friday is Zuhr, but when one attends Jumu'ah, Jumu'ah becomes obligatory and invalidates or transforms the prior Zuhr depending on the presence of an excuse.

Invalidation of Zuhr Prayer by Intending Friday Prayer: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explains invalidation of Zuhr upon attending Friday prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi positions on when a Zuhr prayer is invalidated by attending Friday prayer, distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa, the Sahibayn, and Zufar, and apply the ruling to excused and unexcused persons.

Core Idea
Setting out for Friday invalidates the Zuhr of an unexcused person; for an excused person, it becomes nafl.

In this passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira, the author discusses a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: at what point does intending to attend Friday prayer invalidate a previously performed Zuhr? The answer depends on whether the person has a valid excuse and which Imam's view we follow. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, the rational dalil, and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the three positions
Recognize that the issue is about a person who has already prayed Zuhr and then intends to attend Friday. Note the three positions: Abu Hanifa (invalidation upon setting out), Abu Yusuf & Muhammad (invalidation upon entering with the imam), and Zufar (no invalidation for the excused).
2
Distinguish excused vs. unexcused
Understand that the ruling differs based on whether the person had a valid excuse (travel, sickness, slavery) at the time of Zuhr. For the unexcused, Zuhr becomes batil; for the excused, it converts to nafl.
The excused person, if he attends Friday, becomes like the unexcused.
3
Analyze the rational dalil
The author argues that Friday is stronger than Zuhr due to its additional conditions (congregation, khutba, residence). The weaker (Zuhr) cannot stand against the stronger, so intending Friday invalidates Zuhr.
This is a dalil aqli, not a textual proof.
4
Explore the thamarah al-khilaf
Consider the practical scenario: If an excused person prays Zuhr at home, then joins Friday, and before the imam completes Friday, Zuhr time ends – according to the majority he must repeat Zuhr; Zufar says no repetition.
The consequence is whether a second Zuhr is required.
5
Consider additional scenarios
Examine the nuances: setting out after the imam has finished, setting out but not catching the prayer due to distance (Balkhi vs. Iraqi view), and simultaneous departure and completion.
These refine the application of the ruling.

وقوله: ولا عُذر به فلو كان به عُذر من الأعذار التي ذكرناها، فصلى الظهر، ثم شهد الجمعة : كانت الجمعة فرضه عندنا، وانقلب ظهره نفلاً؛ لأنه إذا شهدها: فهو والصحيح سواء.

فوقعت الظهر موقع الفرض من غير إعادة.

للظهر، ولا يظهر الضعيف في مقابلة القوي.

الظهر عند أبي حنيفة بالسعي).
فإن صلى الجمعة: أجزأته، وإن لم يصلها: أعاد الظهر.
وقالا : لا تبطل صلاة الظهر حتى يدخل مع الإمام .

Chapter of Friday Prayer. If he intends to attend Friday, and sets out for it, the Zuhr prayer becomes invalid according to Abu Hanifa by his going. And his statement: 'And there is no excuse for him.' So if he has an excuse from the excuses we mentioned, and he prayed Zuhr, then attended Friday: Friday is his obligation according to us, and his Zuhr becomes supererogatory; because when he attends it, he and the healthy person are equal. Zufar said: His obligation is Zuhr, and it is not annulled; because Friday is not obligatory upon him, so Zuhr occupies the position of obligation without repetition. The benefit is: if the excused person or the slave prayed Zuhr in his home, then entered into Friday with the imam, and before the imam completes Friday the time of Zuhr exits: according to us, he must repeat Zuhr because his first Zuhr becomes supererogatory. According to Zufar, he does not have to repeat; because this day in his regard is like other days, and in other days if he prayed Zuhr at home then prayed it with congregation, his obligation is what he performed at home – likewise this. But we say: Friday is stronger than Zuhr; because conditions are required for it that are not required for Zuhr, and the weak does not appear in opposition to the strong. His statement: ('If he intends to attend Friday, and sets out for it, the Zuhr prayer becomes invalid according to Abu Hanifa by his going.') If he prays Friday, it suffices him; if he does not pray it, he repeats Zuhr. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: The Zuhr prayer is not invalidated until he enters with the imam. The slave, the sick, the traveler and others are equal in the invalidation by going. Thus in al-Musaffa. This is when he goes to it while the imam is in prayer, or before he prays. But if he goes to it when the imam has already prayed it, his Zuhr is not invalidated. [yarju?] And in al-Nihaya: If he goes before the imam prays it, but he cannot catch it due to distance, his Zuhr is not invalidated according to the Iraqis, and it is invalidated according to the Balkhans, and that is correct. And if he sets out for it before the imam prays it, then the imam did not pray it, due to an excuse or otherwise, they differed about the invalidation of his Zuhr, and the correct view is that it is not invalidated. Thus in al-Nihaya. And if his departure and the imam's completion are simultaneous, his Zuhr is not invalidated. And if he had prayed with congregation and sets out for it, his Zuhr is invalidated but not theirs. * His statement: (And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: It is not invalidated until he enters with the imam.) In it there is an indication that completion is not a condition for the invalidation of Zuhr according to them. And Shaykh al-Islam mentioned that according to their statement, his Zuhr is not invalidated until he performs all of Friday.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of intending to attend Friday prayer on a previously performed Zuhr prayer. The text opens with the matn from al-Quduri: if a person who has no valid excuse (like travel or sickness) decides to attend Friday and sets out for it, his Zuhr prayer becomes invalid (bāṭil) according to Abu Hanifa – even if he only began walking towards the mosque. The commentary then clarifies that if a person had a valid excuse (like being a traveler, sick, or a slave) and initially prayed Zuhr at home, then later attended Friday, that person's Friday becomes obligatory and his Zuhr converts to a supererogatory (nafl) prayer. This is because Friday, being stronger, overrides the Zuhr. The distinction between the excused and unexcused is crucial: the unexcused person's Zuhr becomes invalid (bātil) and must be repeated if he does not complete Friday; the excused person's Zuhr becomes nafl and he does not need to repeat anything if he completes Friday. The text then presents Zufar's dissenting view: Zufar argues that the original Zuhr remains obligatory and is not annulled, and that even if the excused person later joins Friday, his Zuhr is still his duty. The practical benefit (fā’ida) is illustrated: if an excused person or slave prays Zuhr at home, then joins the imam in Friday prayer, and before the imam completes the prayer, the time of Zuhr ends – according to the majority (Abu Hanifa), that person must repeat Zuhr because his first Zuhr became nafl; Zufar says no repetition is needed. The author then provides a rational argument (dalīl ‘aqlī): Friday is stronger than Zuhr because it has additional conditions (like congregation, khutba, and condition of residence). Therefore, the weaker (Zuhr) cannot stand against the stronger (Friday). Next, the text returns to the matn and adds: if the person actually prays Friday, that suffices; if he does not, he must repeat Zuhr. Then the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sāhibayn) are given: they hold that the Zuhr is not invalidated simply by setting out; rather, it is invalidated only when the person actually joins the imam in prayer (enters the congregation). The commentary clarifies that according to them, completion of Friday is not a condition for the invalidation – just entering with the imam suffices. However, Shaykh al-Islam (likely a later authority) is quoted as saying that according to the Sāhibayn, the Zuhr is not invalidated until the entire Friday prayer is performed. This shows a internal disagreement among those transmitting their view. The passage then discusses various scenarios: The ruling applies equally to the slave, sick, traveler, etc. – their Zuhr is invalidated by setting out. However, if the imam has already finished the Friday prayer when the person sets out, his Zuhr is not invalidated. A dispute between the scholars of Iraq and Balkh is mentioned: if a person sets out but due to distance cannot catch the prayer, the Iraqis say his Zuhr is not invalidated, while the Balkhans say it is; the text declares the Balkhan view as correct (possibly the preferred position). Also, if the person sets out but then for some reason the imam does not pray Friday at all, the correct view is that his Zuhr is not invalidated. If his departure coincides exactly with the imam’s completion, his Zuhr remains valid. An interesting point: if a person prays Zuhr in congregation at home and then sets out for Friday, his own Zuhr is invalidated, but that of his fellow congregants is not – because the invalidation is specific to him due to his intention. The overall relied-upon position (muftā bihī) in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa: setting out invalidates the Zuhr, though some later authorities prefer the view of the Sāhibayn. This passage demonstrates how later Hanafi scholars (like the authors of al-Musaffā and al-Nihāya) refined and clarified the positions, sometimes identifying the stronger opinion.

Key Terms
السعي (al-saiy): Setting out or walking towards the Friday prayer location.
الانتقاض (al-intiqād): The invalidation or breaking of the previous prayer.
المعذور (al-ma‘dhūr): A person with a valid excuse excusing him from Friday prayer, such as travel, sickness, slavery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, at what point does a person's Zuhr prayer become invalid if he intends to attend Friday prayer?
A) When he actually joins the imam in Friday prayer.
B) When he sets out (walks) towards the Friday prayer.
C) When he decides in his heart to attend, even without moving.
D) Only after he completes the Friday prayer.

Key Takeaway
The strength of Friday prayer overrides a previously performed Zuhr, but the exact moment of invalidation and the effect on excused persons differ among Hanafi imams, with the majority view favoring setting out as the trigger.

Invalidation of Dhuhr When Jumu'ah Is Not Caught: Iraqi vs. Balkh Views
Disagreement on invalidation when imam does not pray or person fails to catch due to distance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Iraqi and Balkhian positions on invalidation of Dhuhr when one sets out for Jumu'ah but does not catch it, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Setting out for Jumu'ah does not automatically invalidate Dhuhr; actual catching or imam's prayer matters.

This lesson examines a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the effect of intending Jumu'ah on one's Dhuhr prayer. The passage presents two regional views: the Iraqis hold that setting out does not invalidate Dhuhr unless the prayer is actually caught, while the Balkhians argue that the mere intention to attend Jumu'ah invalidates the Dhuhr. Understanding this difference is crucial for correctly applying the rules of Jumu'ah in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Issue
Recognize that the dispute is about whether the act of setting out (sa'y) for Jumu'ah invalidates the Dhuhr prayer that one would otherwise pray at home.
2
State the Iraqi Position
The Iraqi Hanafis say that setting out does not invalidate Dhuhr; it remains valid and can be prayed later if Jumu'ah is not caught.
3
State the Balkhian Position
The Balkh Hanafis say that setting out invalidates Dhuhr, requiring one to pray Dhuhr after the Jumu'ah time if the Jumu'ah is not caught.
4
Analyze the Second Scenario
If the imam does not pray Jumu'ah at all (for any reason), the correct view (according to the text) is that Dhuhr is not invalidated, regardless of whether the person set out.
This applies even if the person had set out; the condition for invalidation is the actual occurrence of Jumu'ah prayer.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The commentator states that the Balkh view is correct ('wa huwa al-sahih') for the first scenario. For the second scenario, the correct view is that Dhuhr remains valid.
The mufta bihi thus differentiates based on whether the imam actually prays or not.

Source Text

ويرجأ: وفي النهاية : إذا سعى قبل أن يصليها الإمام إلا أنه لا إدراكها لبعد المسافة : لم يبطل ظهره عند العراقيين، ويبطل عند البلخيين، وهو الصحيح. ولو توجه إليها قبل أن يصليها الإمام، ثم إن الإمام لم يصلها، لعذر أو لغير عذر اختلفوا في بطلان ظهره والصحيح أنه لا يبطل. كذا في «النهاية».
And it is hoped: In al-Nihayah: If he set out before the imam prayed it (Jumu'ah) but does not catch it due to distance, his Dhuhr is not invalidated according to the Iraqis, but it is invalid according to the Balkhians, and that (the Balkhian view) is correct. And if he went to it before the imam prayed it, then the imam did not pray it, whether for an excuse or without excuse, they disagreed about the invalidation of his Dhuhr, and the correct view is that it is not invalid. Thus in al-Nihayah.

Commentary
This passage presents a disagreement within the Hanafi school between the scholars of Iraq and those of Balkh regarding a person who sets out for Jumu'ah but does not actually catch the prayer due to distance. According to the Iraqi Hanafis, his Dhuhr remains valid (not invalidated), while the Balkh Hanafis hold that it is invalidated, and the commentator states that the Balkh view is correct. Then a related case: if one goes before the imam prays, but then the imam does not perform Jumu'ah at all (due to an excuse or otherwise), there is disagreement about whether his Dhuhr is invalidated. The correct view, according to the commentator citing al-Nihayah, is that it is not invalid. The underlying principle is that invalidation of Dhuhr depends on either actually performing Jumu'ah or at least a valid intention to attend that materializes in a prayer that takes place. If the imam does not pray, the condition for invalidation is not met.

Key Terms
إدراك (idrāk): Catching or attaining the Friday prayer, i.e., joining the imam in at least one rak'ah.
العراقيون والبلخيون (al-‘Irāqiyyūn wa al-Balkhiyyūn): Two regional groups of Hanafi scholars: the Iraqis (Kufa/Baghdad) and the Balkhians (Central Asia).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct view (mufta bihi) in this passage, if a person sets out for Jumu'ah but the imam does not perform the prayer at all, what is the status of his Dhuhr?
A) His Dhuhr is invalidated and he must pray Dhuhr again.
B) His Dhuhr remains valid and he does not need to repeat it.
C) His Dhuhr becomes invalid only if he had a bad intention.
D) He must combine Dhuhr with 'Asr.

Key Takeaway
The correct view is that Dhuhr is not invalidated when the imam does not pray, because the condition for invalidation (actual Jumu'ah prayer) is not met.

Simultaneous Departure and Prior Congregation Cases
Rulings on simultaneous departure and prior congregation for Jumu'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two scenarios described in the passage and state the correct Hanafi ruling for each scenario, explaining why the invalidation applies only to the individual who goes.

Core Idea
Going to Jumu'ah after Dhuhr in congregation invalidates your Dhuhr alone, not your companions'.

This passage from al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah presents two nuanced rulings regarding the interaction between Dhuhr and Jumu'ah. The first addresses timing: if you set out for Jumu'ah exactly when the imam finishes, your Dhuhr remains valid. The second addresses prior congregation: if you already prayed Dhuhr in a group and then head to Jumu'ah, your Dhuhr becomes invalid, but your companions' Dhuhr is unaffected. These rulings highlight the precision of Hanafi fiqh in handling overlapping acts of worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and note the two distinct cases: (1) simultaneous departure with the imam's completion, and (2) praying Dhuhr in congregation then going to Jumu'ah.
2
State the ruling for scenario 1
The passage says 'لم يبطل ظهره' – his Dhuhr is not invalidated. Understand that this holds because the imam has already finished; the obligation of Jumu'ah has been fulfilled for the community.
3
State the ruling for scenario 2 for the individual
The passage says 'بطلت الظهر في حقه' – the Dhuhr is invalidated with respect to him. This is because by going to Jumu'ah he shows preference for Jumu'ah, which retroactively nullifies his Dhuhr.
4
State the ruling for scenario 2 for the companions
The passage adds 'ولم تبطل في حقهم' – the Dhuhr is not invalidated with respect to them. Their prayers remain valid because they did not share his intention to attend Jumu'ah.
This distinction prevents the invalidation of a valid congregation due to a subsequent individual act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان خروجه وفراغ الإمام معاً: لم يبطل ظهره. ولو كان قد صلى بجماعة، وتوجه إليها بطلت الظهر في حقه، ولم تبطل في حقهم.
And if his departure and the imam's finishing were simultaneous, his Dhuhr is not invalidated. And if he had prayed in congregation [the Dhuhr prayer] and then went to it (Jumu'ah), the Dhuhr is invalidated with respect to him, but not with respect to them (his companions).
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage states two independent rules. First, if a person sets out for Jumu'ah at the exact moment the imam finishes the Jumu'ah prayer (i.e., the imam has already completed it), then his Dhuhr prayer remains valid and is not invalidated. This is because the condition of invalidation is that the imam is still praying or has not yet prayed; once the imam has finished, going does not affect Dhuhr. Second, if a person had already performed his Dhuhr prayer in congregation (with others) and then subsequently heads to attend Jumu'ah, his own Dhuhr becomes invalid (because he intended Jumu'ah after performing Dhuhr), but the Dhuhr of those who prayed with him remains valid. This distinction is important: his individual intention nullifies his own prayer, but the validity of others' prayers is not affected by his subsequent act.

Key Terms
فراغ الإمام (farāgh al-imām): The completion of the imam's Friday prayer, including the taslim.
بطلت الظهر في حقه (baṭalat al-ẓuhr fī ḥaqqih): The Dhuhr prayer is invalidated with respect to that individual, meaning he must now intend Jumu'ah instead.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person prays Dhuhr in congregation and then, after the prayer, sets out for Jumu'ah, what is the ruling on his Dhuhr and the Dhuhr of his companions?
A) Both his and his companions' Dhuhr are invalidated.
B) His Dhuhr is invalidated, but his companions' Dhuhr remains valid.
C) His Dhuhr remains valid, and his companions' Dhuhr is invalidated.
D) Neither his nor his companions' Dhuhr is affected.

Key Takeaway
When a person goes to Jumu'ah after having prayed Dhuhr in congregation, only his own Dhuhr is invalidated; his companions' prayers remain valid.
